<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Food</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Substances consumed for nutrition</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Food_(disambiguation)" title="Food (disambiguation)">Food (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Foodstuffs" redirects here. For the New Zealand grocery company, see <a href="/wiki/Foodstuffs_(company)" title="Foodstuffs (company)">Foodstuffs (company)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Split plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="60" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that  this article should be <b><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a></b> into articles titled <i><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Food</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_food" title="Human food">Human food</a></i>. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Food" title="Talk:Food">discuss</a>)  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg"><img alt="Table set with red meat, bread, pasta, vegetables, fruit, fish, and beans" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg/330px-Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg/495px-Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg/660px-Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Good_Food_Display_-_NCI_Visuals_Online.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Display of various foods</div></div></div>
<p><b>Food</b> is any substance consumed to provide <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutritional</a> support for an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a>. Food is usually of <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungal</a> origin, and contains essential <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">proteins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">minerals</a>. The substance is <a href="/wiki/Ingestion" title="Ingestion">ingested</a> by an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a> and assimilated by the organism's <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> to provide <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, maintain life, or stimulate growth. Different species of animals have different <a href="/wiki/List_of_feeding_behaviours" title="List of feeding behaviours">feeding behaviours</a> that satisfy the needs of their unique <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolisms</a>, often evolved to fill a specific <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a> within specific geographical contexts.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">Omnivorous</a> humans are highly adaptable and have adapted to obtain food in many different ecosystems. Historically, <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> secured food through two main methods: <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunting and gathering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. As agricultural technologies increased, humans settled into <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_society" title="Agrarian society">agriculture lifestyles</a> with diets shaped by the agriculture opportunities in their geography. Geographic and cultural differences has led to creation of numerous <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">cuisines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Culinary_arts" title="Culinary arts">culinary arts</a>, including a wide array of <a href="/wiki/Ingredient" title="Ingredient">ingredients</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culinary_herb" title="Culinary herb">herbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spices</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_cooking_techniques" title="List of cooking techniques">techniques</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dish_(food)" title="Dish (food)">dishes</a>. As cultures have mixed through forces like <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">international trade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>, ingredients have become more widely available beyond their geographic and cultural origins, creating a cosmopolitan exchange of different food traditions and practices.
</p><p>Today, the majority of the <a href="/wiki/Food_energy" title="Food energy">food energy</a> required by the ever-<a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">increasing</a> <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">population of the world</a> is supplied by the industrial <a href="/wiki/Food_industry" title="Food industry">food industry</a>, which produces food with <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive agriculture</a> and distributes it through complex <a href="/wiki/Food_processing" title="Food processing">food processing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_distribution" title="Food distribution">food distribution</a> systems. This system of conventional agriculture relies heavily on <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>, which means that the food and agricultural system is one of the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture" title="Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture">major contributors to climate change</a>, accountable for as much as 37% of total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse-gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse-gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Addressing the <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">carbon intensity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Food_system" title="Food system">food system</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_waste" title="Food waste">food waste</a> are <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">important mitigation measures</a> in the global response to climate change.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The food system has significant impacts on a wide range of other social and political issues including: <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_diversity" title="Biological diversity">biological diversity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutritional_economics" title="Nutritional economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">water supply</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">access to food</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Right_to_food" title="Right to food">right to food</a> is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_right" title="Human right">human right</a> derived from the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Economic,_Social_and_Cultural_Rights" title="International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights">International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ICESCR" title="ICESCR">ICESCR</a>), recognizing the "right to an adequate <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>, including adequate food", as well as the "<a href="/wiki/Fundamental_rights" title="Fundamental rights">fundamental right</a> to be free from <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a>". Because of these fundamental rights, <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> is often a priority international policy activity; for example <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_2" title="Sustainable Development Goal 2">Sustainable Development Goal 2</a> "Zero hunger" is meant to eliminate <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a> by 2030. <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">Food safety</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> are monitored by international agencies like the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_for_Food_Protection" title="International Association for Food Protection">International Association for Food Protection</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Resources_Institute" title="World Resources Institute">World Resources Institute</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Food_Programme" title="World Food Programme">World Food Programme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/International_Food_Information_Council" title="International Food Information Council">International Food Information Council</a>, and are often subject to national regulation by institutions, like the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> in the United States.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition_and_classification">Definition and classification</span></h2>
<p>Food is any substance consumed to provide <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutritional</a> support and energy to an <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3">[3]</a></sup> It can be raw, processed or formulated and is consumed orally by animals for growth, health or pleasure. Food is mainly composed of water, <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>. <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">Minerals</a> (e.g. salts) and organic substances (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Vitamin" title="Vitamin">vitamins</a>) can also be found in food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Plants, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and some microorganisms use <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> to make their own food molecules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Water is found in many foods and has been defined as a food by itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>  Water and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">fiber</a> have low energy densities, or <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a>, while fat is the most energy dense component.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-3">[3]</a></sup> Some inorganic (non-food) elements are also essential for plant and animal functioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Human food can be classified in various ways, either by related content or by how the food is processed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The number and composition of <a href="/wiki/Food_group" title="Food group">food groups</a> can vary. Most systems include four basic groups that describe their origin and relative nutritional function: Vegetables and Fruit, Cereals and Bread, Dairy, and Meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Studies that look into diet quality often group food into whole grains/cereals, refined grains/cereals, vegetables, fruits, nuts, legumes, eggs, dairy products, fish, red meat, processed meat, and sugar-sweetened beverages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> use a system with nineteen food classifications: cereals, roots, pulses and nuts, milk, eggs, fish and shellfish, meat, insects, vegetables, fruits, fats and oils, sweets and sugars, spices and condiments, beverages, foods for nutritional uses, food additives, composite dishes and savoury snacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_sources">Food sources</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aquatic_food_web.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1176" data-file-width="895" decoding="async" height="289" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Aquatic_food_web.jpg/220px-Aquatic_food_web.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Aquatic_food_web.jpg/330px-Aquatic_food_web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Aquatic_food_web.jpg/440px-Aquatic_food_web.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aquatic_food_web.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical aquatic food web</div></div></div>
<p>In a given ecosystem, food forms a <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">web</a> of interlocking <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">chains</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_producers" title="Primary producers">primary producers</a> at the bottom and <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predators</a> at the top.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup> Other aspects of the web include <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detrovores</a> (that eat <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritis</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Decomposer" title="Decomposer">decomposers</a> (that break down dead organisms).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14">[14]</a></sup>  Primary producers include algae, plants, bacteria and protists that acquire their energy from sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Primary consumers are the herbivores that consume the pants and secondary consumers are the carnivores that consume those herbivores. Some organisms, including most mammals and birds, diets consist of both animals and plants and they are considered omnivores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup> The chain ends with the apex predators, the animals that have no known predators in its ecosystem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Humans are often considered apex predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans are omnivores finding sustenance in vegetables, fruits, cooked meat, milk, eggs, mushrooms and seaweed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">Cereal</a> grain is a <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> that provides more food energy worldwide than any other type of crop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">Corn (maize)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>  account for 87% of all grain production worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-prodstat_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-prodstat-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Just over half of the worlds crops are used to feed humans (55 percent), with 36 percent grown as animal feed and 9 percent for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biofuels" title="Biofuels">biofuels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Fungi and bacteria are also used in the preparation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermentation_(food)" title="Fermentation (food)">fermented</a> foods like bread, wine, <a href="/wiki/Cheese" title="Cheese">cheese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg/220px-Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg/330px-Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg/440px-Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Yoghurt_in_a_steel_vessel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yoghurt in a steel vessel</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunlight">Sunlight</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">Photosynthesis</a> is the ultimate source of energy and food for nearly all life on earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> It is the main food source for plants, algae and certain bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Without this all organisms which depend on these organisms further up the food chain would be unable to exist, from coral to lions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Energy from the sun is absorbed and used to transform water and carbon dioxide in the air or soil into oxygen and glucose. The oxygen is then released and the glucose stored as an energy reserve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plants">Plants</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Foods_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="1793" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Foods_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Foods_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Foods_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Foods_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Foods_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Foods_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Foods_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Foods from plant sources</div></div></div>
<p>Plants as a food source are often divided into seeds, fruits, vegetables, legumes, grains and nuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup> Where plants fall within these categories can vary with botanically described fruits such as the tomato, squash, pepper and eggplant or seeds like peas commonly considered vegetables.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Food is a fruit if the part eaten is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(botany)" title="Ovary (botany)">reproductive tissue</a>, so seeds, nuts and grains are technically fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-32">[32]</a></sup> From a culinary perspective fruits are generally considered the remains of botanically described fruits after grains, nuts, seeds and fruits used as vegetables are removed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-33">[33]</a></sup> Grains can be defined as seeds that humans eat or harvest, with cereal grains (oats, wheat, rice, corn, barley, rye, sorghum and millet) belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Poaceae</a> (grass) family<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and pulses coming from the <a href="/wiki/Fabaceae" title="Fabaceae">Fabaceae</a> (legume) family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Whole_grain" title="Whole grain">Whole grains</a> are foods that contain all the elements of the original seed (bran, germ, and <a href="/wiki/Endosperm" title="Endosperm">endosperm</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Nuts are dry fruits distinguishable by their woody shell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-:8-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fleshy fruits (distinguishable from dry fruits like grain, seeds and nuts) can be further classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_fruits" title="Stone fruits">stone fruits</a> (cherries and peaches), <a href="/wiki/Pome" title="Pome">pome fruits</a> (apples, pears), <a href="/wiki/Berry" title="Berry">berries</a>  (blackberry, strawberry),  <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a> (oranges, lemon), <a href="/wiki/Melon" title="Melon">melons</a> (watermelon, cantaloupe), Mediterranean fruits (grapes, fig), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_fruits" title="Tropical fruits">tropical fruits</a> (banana, pineapple).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-:8-33">[33]</a></sup> Vegetables refer to any other part of the plant that can be eaten, including roots, stems, leaves, flowers, bark or the entire plant itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-37">[37]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Root_vegetable" title="Root vegetable">root vegetables</a> (<a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carrot" title="Carrot">carrots</a>), bulbs (<a href="/wiki/Onion" title="Onion">onion</a> family), flowers (cauliflower and broccoli), <a href="/wiki/Leaf_vegetable" title="Leaf vegetable">leaf vegetables</a> (<a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lettuce" title="Lettuce">lettuce</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Category:Stem_vegetables" title="Category:Stem vegetables">stem vegetables</a> (celery and <a href="/wiki/Asparagus" title="Asparagus">asparagus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plants have high <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> content, with carbohydrates mainly in the form of starch, fructose, glucose and other sugars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup>  Most vitamins are found from plant sources, with the notable exceptions of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a>. <a href="/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient)" title="Mineral (nutrient)">Minerals</a> are also plentiful, although the presence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytates" title="Phytates">phytates</a> can prevent their release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup>  Fruit can consist of up to 90% water, contain high levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_sugars" title="Simple sugars">simple sugars</a> that contribute to their sweet taste and have a high <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-:8-33">[33]</a></sup> Compared to fleshy fruit (excepting Bananas) vegetables are high in starch,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folate" title="Folate">folate</a> and vitamins and low in fat and calories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Grains are more starch based<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup> and nuts have a high protein, fibre, vitamin E and B content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-:8-33">[33]</a></sup> Seeds are a good source of food for animals because they are abundant and contain fibre and healthful fats, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omega-3" title="Omega-3">omega-3 fats</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Animals that only eat plants are called <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a>; with those that mostly just eat fruits known as <a href="/wiki/Frugivore" title="Frugivore">frugivores</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> leaves, while shoot eaters are <a href="/wiki/Folivore" title="Folivore">folivores</a> (pandas) and wood eaters termed <a href="/wiki/Xylophagy" title="Xylophagy">xylophages</a> (termites).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>  Frugivores include a diverse range of species from annelids to elephants, chimpanzees and many birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> About 182 fish consume seeds or fruit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> There are many types of grasses, adapted to different locations, that animals (domesticated and wild) use as their main source of nutrients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Humans only eat about 200 out of the worlds 400 000 plant species, despite at least half of them being edible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-50">[50]</a></sup> Most human plant-based food comes from maize, rice, and wheat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-50">[50]</a></sup> Plants can be processed into breads, pasta, cereals, juices and jams or raw ingredients such as sugar, herbs, spices and oils can be extracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_29-5"><a href="#cite_note-:0-29">[29]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oilseed" title="Oilseed">Oilseeds</a> are often pressed to produce rich oils - <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_oil" title="Sunflower oil">sunflower</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flaxseed" title="Flaxseed">flaxseed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a> (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canola_oil" title="Canola oil">canola oil</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Sesame_oil" title="Sesame oil">sesame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGee9_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGee9-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many plants and animals have <a href="/wiki/Coevolution" title="Coevolution">coevolved</a> in such a way that the fruit is good source of nutrition to the animal who then excretes the seeds some distance away allowing greater dispersal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Even <a href="/wiki/Seed_predation" title="Seed predation">seed predation</a> can be mutually beneficial as some seeds can survive the digestion process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Insects are major eaters of seeds,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-41">[41]</a></sup> with ants being the only real seed dispersers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Birds, although being major dispersers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> only rarely eat seeds as a source of food and can be identified by their thick beak that is used to crack open the seed coat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Mammals eat a more diverse range of seeds as they are able to crush harder and larger seeds with their teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animals">Animals</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meatfoodgroup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="255" data-file-width="756" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Meatfoodgroup.jpg/330px-Meatfoodgroup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Meatfoodgroup.jpg/495px-Meatfoodgroup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Meatfoodgroup.jpg/660px-Meatfoodgroup.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meatfoodgroup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various raw <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meats</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Animal_source_foods" title="Animal source foods">Animals are used as food</a> either directly or indirectly. This includes meat, eggs, shellfish and dairy products like milk and cheese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> They are an important source or protein and are considered complete proteins for human consumption as they contain all the essential amino acids that the human body needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-60">[60]</a></sup> One 4-ounce (110Â g) steak, chicken breast or pork chop contains about 30 grams of protein. One large egg has 7 grams of protein, a 4-ounce (110Â g) serving of cheese about 15 grams and 1 cup of milk about 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-:9-60">[60]</a></sup> Other nutrients found in animal products include calories, fat, essential vitamins (including B12) and minerals (including zinc, iron, calcium, magnesium).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-:9-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Food products produced by animals include <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a> produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammary_glands" title="Mammary glands">mammary glands</a>, which in many cultures is drunk or processed into <a href="/wiki/Dairy_product" title="Dairy product">dairy products</a> (cheese, butter, etc.). In addition, birds and other animals lay <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a>, which are often eaten, and <a href="/wiki/Bee" title="Bee">bees</a> produce <a href="/wiki/Honey" title="Honey">honey</a>, a reduced <a href="/wiki/Nectar" title="Nectar">nectar</a> from flowers, which is a popular sweetener in many cultures. Some cultures <a href="/wiki/Blood_as_food" title="Blood as food">consume blood</a>, sometimes in the form of <a href="/wiki/Blood_sausage" title="Blood sausage">blood sausage</a>, as a thickener for sauces, or in a <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">cured</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salting_(food)" title="Salting (food)">salted</a> form for times of food scarcity, and others use <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> in stews such as <a href="/wiki/Jugging" title="Jugging">jugged hare</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some cultures and people do not consume meat or animal food products for cultural, dietary, health, ethical, or ideological reasons. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">Vegetarians</a> choose to forgo food from animal sources to varying degrees. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegan" title="Vegan">Vegans</a> do not consume any foods that are or contain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ingredients" title="Ingredients">ingredients</a> from an animal source.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taste_perception">Taste perception</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">Taste</a></div>
<p>Animals, specifically humans, have five different types of tastes: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweet" title="Sweet">sweet</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sour" title="Sour">sour</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltiness" title="Saltiness">salty</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)" title="Bitter (taste)">bitter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">umami</a>. As animals have <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a>, the tastes that provide the most energy (<a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fats</a>) are the most pleasant to eat while others, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)" title="Bitter (taste)">bitter</a>, are not enjoyable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Water, while important for survival, has no taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Fats, on the other hand, especially <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fats</a>, are thicker and rich and are thus considered more enjoyable to eat.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sweet">Sweet</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saccharose2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="178" data-file-width="345" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Saccharose2.svg/200px-Saccharose2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Saccharose2.svg/300px-Saccharose2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Saccharose2.svg/400px-Saccharose2.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saccharose2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Structure of sucrose</div></div></div>
<p>Generally regarded as the most pleasant taste, <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweetness</a> is almost always caused by a type of simple <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> or <a href="/wiki/Fructose" title="Fructose">fructose</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disaccharides" title="Disaccharides">disaccharides</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, a molecule combining glucose and fructose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-New_Oxford_American_Dictionary_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-New_Oxford_American_Dictionary-64">[64]</a></sup> Complex carbohydrates are long chains and thus do not have the sweet taste. Artificial sweeteners such as <a href="/wiki/Sucralose" title="Sucralose">sucralose</a> are used to mimic the sugar molecule, creating the sensation of sweet, without the calories. Other types of sugar include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raw_sugar" title="Raw sugar">raw sugar</a>, which is known for its amber color, as it is unprocessed. As sugar is vital for energy and survival, the taste of sugar is pleasant.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Stevia" title="Stevia">stevia</a> plant contains a compound known as <a href="/wiki/Steviol" title="Steviol">steviol</a> which, when extracted, has 300 times the sweetness of sugar while having minimal impact on blood sugar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sour">Sour</span></h3>
<p>Sourness is caused by the taste of <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Vinegar" title="Vinegar">vinegar</a> in alcoholic beverages. Sour foods include <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">citrus</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Lemon" title="Lemon">lemons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lime_(fruit)" title="Lime (fruit)">limes</a>, and to a lesser degree <a href="/wiki/Orange_(fruit)" title="Orange (fruit)">oranges</a>. Sour is evolutionarily significant as it is a sign for a food that may have gone <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rancidity" title="Rancidity">rancid</a> due to bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Many foods, however, are slightly acidic, and help stimulate the taste buds and enhance flavor.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salty">Salty</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1320" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg/220px-Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg/330px-Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg/440px-Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Piles_of_Salt_Salar_de_Uyuni_Bolivia_Luca_Galuzzi_2006_a.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Salt mounds in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltiness" title="Saltiness">Saltiness</a> is the taste of <a href="/wiki/Alkali_metal" title="Alkali metal">alkali metal</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ions" title="Ions">ions</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sodium" title="Sodium">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>. It is found in almost every food in low to moderate proportions to enhance flavor, although to eat pure salt is regarded as highly unpleasant. There are many different types of salt, with each having a different degree of saltiness, including <a href="/wiki/Sea_salt" title="Sea salt">sea salt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fleur_de_sel" title="Fleur de sel">fleur de sel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kosher_salt" title="Kosher salt">kosher salt</a>, mined salt, and grey salt. Other than enhancing flavor, its significance is that the body needs and maintains a delicate <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> balance, which is the <a href="/wiki/Kidney" title="Kidney">kidney</a>'s function. Salt may be iodized, meaning <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a> has been added to it, a necessary nutrient that promotes <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">thyroid</a> function. Some canned foods, notably <a href="/wiki/Soup" title="Soup">soups</a> or packaged <a href="/wiki/Broth" title="Broth">broths</a>, tend to be high in salt as a means of preserving the food longer. Historically salt has long been used as a meat preservative as salt promotes water excretion. Similarly, dried foods also promote food safety.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bitter">Bitter</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bitter_(taste)" title="Bitter (taste)">Bitterness</a> is a sensation often considered unpleasant characterized by having a sharp, pungent taste. Unsweetened dark <a href="/wiki/Chocolate" title="Chocolate">chocolate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caffeine" title="Caffeine">caffeine</a>, lemon rind, and some types of fruit are known to be bitter.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Umami">Umami</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">Umami</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Umami&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="theader">Umami</div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Soy_sauce_with_wasabi.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Soy_sauce_with_wasabi.jpg/300px-Soy_sauce_with_wasabi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Soy_sauce_with_wasabi.jpg/400px-Soy_sauce_with_wasabi.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ripe_tomatoes.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1368" data-file-width="1824" decoding="async" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Ripe_tomatoes.JPG/300px-Ripe_tomatoes.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Ripe_tomatoes.JPG/400px-Ripe_tomatoes.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Umami_Snack_(41957534).jpeg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1360" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Umami_Snack_%2841957534%29.jpeg/200px-Umami_Snack_%2841957534%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Umami_Snack_%2841957534%29.jpeg/300px-Umami_Snack_%2841957534%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Umami_Snack_%2841957534%29.jpeg/400px-Umami_Snack_%2841957534%29.jpeg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption"><a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">Soy sauce</a>, ripe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomatoes" title="Tomatoes">tomatoes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miso" title="Miso">miso</a> are examples of foods rich in umami components</div></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Umami" title="Umami">Umami</a> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/uË/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uË</span><span title="/Ë/: primary stress follows">Ë</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ÉË/: 'a' in 'father'">ÉË</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span>/</a></span></span> from <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja">æ¨å³</span> <small>Japanese pronunciation:Â </small><span class="IPA" lang="ja-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Japanese" title="Help:IPA/Japanese">[É¯mami]</a></span>), or savoriness, is one of the five <a href="/wiki/Taste#Basic_tastes" title="Taste">basic tastes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Torii_2013_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Torii_2013-67">[67]</a></sup> It has been described as savory and is characteristic of broths and cooked meats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Fleming_2013_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Fleming_2013-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Jufresa_2015_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Jufresa_2015-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Mouritsen_2014_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Mouritsen_2014-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 35â36">:â35â36â</span></sup>
</p><p>People taste umami through <a href="/wiki/Taste_receptor" title="Taste receptor">taste receptors</a> that typically respond to <a href="/wiki/Glutamic_acid" title="Glutamic acid">glutamates</a> and  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleotides" title="Nucleotides">nucleotides</a>, which are widely present in meat broths and fermented products. Glutamates are commonly added to some foods in the form of <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">monosodium glutamate</a> (MSG), and nucleotides are commonly added in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inosine_monophosphate" title="Inosine monophosphate">inosine monophosphate</a> (IMP) or <a href="/wiki/Guanosine_monophosphate" title="Guanosine monophosphate">guanosine monophosphate</a> (GMP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Nelson_2002_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Nelson_2002-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Delay_2000_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Delay_2000-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Chaudhari_2000_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Chaudhari_2000-74">[74]</a></sup> Since umami has its own receptors rather than arising out of a combination of the traditionally recognized taste receptors, scientists now consider umami to be a distinct taste.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_Torii_2013_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_Torii_2013-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umami_npr_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Umami_npr-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
Foods that have a strong umami flavor include meats, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>, fish (including <a href="/wiki/Fish_sauce" title="Fish sauce">fish sauce</a> and preserved fish such as <a href="/wiki/Maldive_fish" title="Maldive fish">maldive fish</a>, sardines, and anchovies), tomatoes, mushrooms, <a href="/wiki/Hydrolyzed_vegetable_protein" title="Hydrolyzed vegetable protein">hydrolyzed vegetable protein</a>, meat extract, yeast extract, cheeses, and <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>.</div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">Cuisine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Regional_cuisine" title="Regional cuisine">Regional cuisine</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_cuisines" title="Global cuisines">Global cuisines</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bali_cuisine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Bali_cuisine.jpg/220px-Bali_cuisine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Bali_cuisine.jpg/330px-Bali_cuisine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Bali_cuisine.jpg/440px-Bali_cuisine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bali_cuisine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Typical <a href="/wiki/Balinese_cuisine" title="Balinese cuisine">Balinese cuisine</a> in Indonesia</div></div></div>
<p>Many scholars claim that the rhetorical function of food is to represent the culture of a country, and that it can be used as a form of communication. According to Goode, Curtis and Theophano, food "is the last aspect of an ethnic culture to be lost".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many cultures have a recognizable cuisine, a specific set of <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a> traditions using various spices or a combination of flavors unique to that culture, which evolves over time. Other differences include preferences (hot or cold, spicy, etc.) and practices, the study of which is known as <a href="/wiki/Gastronomy" title="Gastronomy">gastronomy</a>. Many cultures have diversified their foods by means of preparation, cooking methods, and manufacturing. This also includes a complex food trade which helps the cultures to economically survive by way of food, not just by consumption.
</p><p>Some popular types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_food" title="Ethnic food">ethnic foods</a> include <a href="/wiki/Italian_cuisine" title="Italian cuisine">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_cuisine" title="French cuisine">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_cuisine" title="Chinese cuisine">Chinese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_the_United_States" title="Cuisine of the United States">American</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cajun_cuisine" title="Cajun cuisine">Cajun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thai_cuisine" title="Thai cuisine">Thai</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_cuisine" title="African cuisine">African</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_cuisine" title="Indian cuisine">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepalese_cuisine" title="Nepalese cuisine">Nepalese</a>. Various cultures throughout the world study the dietary analysis of food <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habit_(psychology)" title="Habit (psychology)">habits</a>. While <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionarily</a> speaking, as opposed to culturally, humans are <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">omnivores</a>, religion and social constructs such as <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Activism" title="Activism">activism</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a> will often affect which foods they will consume. Food is eaten and typically enjoyed through the sense of <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a>, the perception of flavor from eating and drinking. Certain tastes are more enjoyable than others, for evolutionary purposes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Presentation">Presentation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG/220px-Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG/330px-Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG/440px-Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Terrine_de_saumon_au_basilic.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A French basil salmon <a href="/wiki/Terrine_(food)" title="Terrine (food)">terrine</a>, with eye-appealing <a href="/wiki/Garnish_(food)" title="Garnish (food)">garnishes</a></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_presentation" title="Food presentation">Food presentation</a></div>
<p>Aesthetically pleasing and eye-appealing food presentations can encourage people to consume foods. A common saying is that people "eat with their eyes". Food presented in a clean and appetizing way will encourage a good flavor, even if unsatisfactory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Food_Texture,_Andrew_J._Rosenthal_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Food_Texture,_Andrew_J._Rosenthal-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Texture_(food)" title="Texture (food)">Texture</a> plays a crucial role in the enjoyment of eating foods. Contrasts in textures, such as something crunchy in an otherwise smooth dish, may increase the appeal of eating it. Common examples include adding <a href="/wiki/Granola" title="Granola">granola</a> to <a href="/wiki/Yogurt" title="Yogurt">yogurt</a>, adding <a href="/wiki/Crouton" title="Crouton">croutons</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Salad" title="Salad">salad</a> or <a href="/wiki/Soup" title="Soup">soup</a>, and toasting bread to enhance its crunchiness for a smooth topping, such as jam or butter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another universal phenomenon regarding food is the appeal of contrast in taste and presentation. For example, such opposite flavors as <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweetness</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saltiness" title="Saltiness">saltiness</a> tend to go well together, as in <a href="/wiki/Kettle_corn" title="Kettle corn">kettle corn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">nuts</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_preparation">Food preparation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_food_preparation" title="Outline of food preparation">Outline of food preparation</a></div>
<p>While many foods can be eaten raw, many also undergo some form of preparation for reasons of safety, <a href="/wiki/Palatability" title="Palatability">palatability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mouthfeel" title="Mouthfeel">texture</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">flavor</a>. At the simplest level this may involve washing, cutting, trimming, or adding other foods or ingredients, such as spices. It may also involve mixing, heating or cooling, <a href="/wiki/Pressure_cooking" title="Pressure cooking">pressure cooking</a>, fermentation, or combination with other food. In a home, most food preparation takes place in a <a href="/wiki/Kitchen" title="Kitchen">kitchen</a>. Some preparation is done to enhance the <a href="/wiki/Taste" title="Taste">taste</a> or aesthetic appeal; other preparation may help to <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">preserve</a> the food; others may be involved in cultural identity. A <a href="/wiki/Meal" title="Meal">meal</a> is made up of food which is prepared to be eaten at a specific time and place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg/220px-LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg/330px-LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg/440px-LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LG_refrigerator_interior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A refrigerator helps to keep foods fresh.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_preparation">Animal preparation</span></h4>
<p>The preparation of animal-based food usually involves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slaughter_(livestock)" title="Slaughter (livestock)">slaughter</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evisceration_(organ_removal)" title="Evisceration (organ removal)">evisceration</a>, hanging, portioning, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rendering_(food_processing)" title="Rendering (food processing)">rendering</a>. In developed countries, this is usually done outside the home in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slaughterhouses" title="Slaughterhouses">slaughterhouses</a>, which are used to process animals en masse for meat production. Many countries regulate their slaughterhouses by law. For example, the United States has established the <a href="/wiki/Humane_Slaughter_Act" title="Humane Slaughter Act">Humane Slaughter Act</a> of 1958, which requires that an animal be stunned before killing. This act, like those in many countries, exempts slaughter in accordance with religious law, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosher" title="Kosher">kosher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shechita" title="Shechita">shechita</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhab%C4%AB%E1%B8%A5ah" title="DhabÄ«á¸¥ah">dhabÄ«á¸¥ah</a> halal. Strict interpretations of <a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">kashrut</a> require the animal to be fully aware when its carotid artery is cut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the local level, a butcher may commonly break down larger animal meat into smaller manageable cuts, and pre-wrap them for commercial sale or wrap them to order in butcher paper. In addition, fish and <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a> may be fabricated into smaller cuts by a fishmonger. However, fish butchery may be done on board a fishing vessel and quick-frozen for the preservation of quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Raw_food_preparation">Raw food preparation<span class="anchor" id="Raw_Food_Preparation"></span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg/220px-2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg/330px-2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg/440px-2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007feb-sushi-odaiba-manytypes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many types of <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> ready to be eaten, including <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a></div></div></div>
<p>Certain cultures highlight animal and vegetable foods in a <a href="/wiki/Raw_foodism" title="Raw foodism">raw state</a>. <a href="/wiki/Salad" title="Salad">Salads</a> consisting of raw vegetables or fruits are common in many cuisines. <a href="/wiki/Sashimi" title="Sashimi">Sashimi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a> consists of raw sliced <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> or other meat, and <a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a> often incorporates raw fish or seafood. <a href="/wiki/Steak_tartare" title="Steak tartare">Steak tartare</a> and salmon tartare are dishes made from diced or ground raw beef or salmon, mixed with various ingredients and served with <a href="/wiki/Baguette" title="Baguette">baguettes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brioche" title="Brioche">brioche</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frites" title="Frites">frites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> In Italy, <a href="/wiki/Carpaccio" title="Carpaccio">carpaccio</a> is a dish of very thinly sliced raw <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>, drizzled with a <a href="/wiki/Vinaigrette" title="Vinaigrette">vinaigrette</a> made with olive oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The health food movement known as <a href="/wiki/Raw_foodism" title="Raw foodism">raw foodism</a> promotes a mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegan" title="Vegan">vegan</a> diet of raw fruits, vegetables, and grains prepared in various ways, including juicing, food dehydration, sprouting, and other methods of preparation that do not heat the food above 118Â Â°F (47.8Â Â°C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> An example of a raw meat dish is <a href="/wiki/Ceviche" title="Ceviche">ceviche</a>, a Latin American dish made with raw meat that is "cooked" from the highly acidic citric juice from lemons and limes along with other aromatics such as garlic.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking">Cooking</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1920" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg/200px-Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg/300px-Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg/400px-Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wok_cooking_and_the_heat_source_by_The_Pocket_in_Nanjing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cooking with a <a href="/wiki/Wok" title="Wok">wok</a> in China</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">Cooking</a></div>
<p>The term "cooking" encompasses a vast range of methods, tools, and combinations of ingredients to improve the flavor or digestibility of food. Cooking technique, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Culinary_art" title="Culinary art">culinary art</a>, generally requires the selection, measurement, and combining of ingredients in an ordered procedure in an effort to achieve the desired result. Constraints on success include the variability of ingredients, ambient conditions, <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tools</a>, and the skill of the individual cook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> The diversity of cooking worldwide is a reflection of the myriad nutritional, aesthetic, agricultural, economic, cultural, and religious considerations that affect it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silhnc_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-silhnc-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cooking requires applying heat to a food which usually, though not always, chemically changes the molecules, thus changing its flavor, <a href="/wiki/Mouthfeel" title="Mouthfeel">texture</a>, appearance, and nutritional properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Cooking certain proteins, such as egg whites, meats, and fish, denatures the protein, causing it to firm. There is archaeological evidence of roasted foodstuffs at <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> campsites dating from 420,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Boiling as a means of cooking requires a container, and has been practiced at least since the 10th millennium BC with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking_equipment">Cooking equipment</span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pfanne_(Edelstahl).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="409" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Pfanne_%28Edelstahl%29.jpg/220px-Pfanne_%28Edelstahl%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Pfanne_%28Edelstahl%29.jpg/330px-Pfanne_%28Edelstahl%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Pfanne_%28Edelstahl%29.jpg/440px-Pfanne_%28Edelstahl%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pfanne_(Edelstahl).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Stainless_steel" title="Stainless steel">stainless steel</a> <a href="/wiki/Frying_pan" title="Frying pan">frying pan</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TraditionalAsado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TraditionalAsado.jpg/220px-TraditionalAsado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TraditionalAsado.jpg/330px-TraditionalAsado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/TraditionalAsado.jpg/440px-TraditionalAsado.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TraditionalAsado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A traditional <a href="/wiki/Asado" title="Asado">asado</a> (barbecue)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cookware_and_bakeware" title="Cookware and bakeware">Cookware and bakeware</a></div>
<p>There are many different types of equipment used for cooking.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Oven" title="Oven">Ovens</a> are mostly hollow devices that get very hot (up to 500Â Â°F (260Â Â°C)) and are used for baking or roasting and offer a dry-heat cooking method. Different cuisines will use different types of ovens. For example, Indian culture uses a <a href="/wiki/Tandoor" title="Tandoor">tandoor</a> oven, which is a cylindrical clay oven which operates at a single high temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Western kitchens use variable temperature <a href="/wiki/Convection_oven" title="Convection oven">convection ovens</a>, conventional ovens, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toaster_oven" title="Toaster oven">toaster ovens</a>, or non-radiant heat ovens like the <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">microwave oven</a>. Classic Italian cuisine includes the use of a brick oven containing burning wood. Ovens may be wood-fired, coal-fired, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">gas</a>, electric, or oil-fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>Various types of cook-tops are used as well. They carry the same variations of fuel types as the ovens mentioned above. Cook-tops are used to heat vessels placed on top of the heat source, such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saut%C3%A9_pan" title="SautÃ© pan">sautÃ© pan</a>, sauce pot, <a href="/wiki/Frying_pan" title="Frying pan">frying pan</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pressure_cooker" title="Pressure cooker">pressure cooker</a>. These pieces of equipment can use either a moist or dry cooking method and include methods such as <a href="/wiki/Steam" title="Steam">steaming</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simmer" title="Simmer">simmering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Poaching_(cooking)" title="Poaching (cooking)">poaching</a> for moist methods, while the dry methods include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saut%C3%A9" title="SautÃ©">sautÃ©ing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan_fry" title="Pan fry">pan frying</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep-fry" title="Deep-fry">deep-frying</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, many cultures use grills for cooking. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grill_(cooking)" title="Grill (cooking)">grill</a> operates with a radiant heat source from below, usually covered with a metal grid and sometimes a cover. An open-pit barbecue in the American south is one example along with the American style outdoor grill fueled by wood, liquid propane, or charcoal along with soaked wood chips for smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Mexican_cuisine" title="Mexican cuisine">Mexican</a> style of barbecue is called <a href="/wiki/Barbacoa" title="Barbacoa">barbacoa</a>, which involves the cooking of meats such as whole sheep over an open fire. In Argentina, an <a href="/wiki/Asado" title="Asado">asado</a> (Spanish for "grilled") is prepared on a grill held over an open pit or fire made upon the ground, on which a whole animal or smaller cuts are grilled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restaurants">Restaurants</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inside_Le_Procope.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="954" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Inside_Le_Procope.jpg/220px-Inside_Le_Procope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Inside_Le_Procope.jpg/330px-Inside_Le_Procope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Inside_Le_Procope.jpg/440px-Inside_Le_Procope.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inside_Le_Procope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Procope" title="CafÃ© Procope">CafÃ© Procope</a> in Paris was founded in 1686</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_(March_5,_1859).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_%28March_5%2C_1859%29.jpg/220px-Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_%28March_5%2C_1859%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_%28March_5%2C_1859%29.jpg/330px-Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_%28March_5%2C_1859%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_%28March_5%2C_1859%29.jpg/440px-Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_%28March_5%2C_1859%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allyn_House_restaurant_menu_(March_5,_1859).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Allyn House restaurant menu (5 March 1859)</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Restaurant" title="Restaurant">Restaurant</a></div>
<p>Restaurants employ <a href="/wiki/Chef" title="Chef">chefs</a> to prepare the food, and <a href="/wiki/Waiting_staff" title="Waiting staff">waiters</a> to serve customers at the table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> The term restaurant comes from an old term for a restorative <a href="/wiki/Broth" title="Broth">meat broth</a>; this broth (or bouillon) was served in elegant outlets in Paris from the mid 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-history-98">[98]</a></sup> These refined "restaurants" were a marked change from the usual basic eateries such as inns and taverns,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-history_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-history-98">[98]</a></sup> and some had developed from early <a href="/wiki/Parisian_caf%C3%A9" title="Parisian cafÃ©">Parisian cafÃ©s</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_Procope" title="CafÃ© Procope">CafÃ© Procope</a>, by first serving bouillon, then adding other cooked food to their menus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commercial eateries existed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_period" title="Roman period">Roman period</a>, with evidence of 150 "thermopolia", a form of fast food restaurant, found in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> and urban sales of prepared foods may have existed in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2005, the population of the United States spent $496 billion on out-of-home dining. Expenditures by type of out-of-home dining were as follows: 40% in full-service restaurants, 37.2% in limited service restaurants (<a href="/wiki/Fast_food" title="Fast food">fast food</a>), 6.6% in schools or colleges, 5.4% in bars and <a href="/wiki/Vending_machine" title="Vending machine">vending machines</a>, 4.7% in hotels and motels, 4.0% in recreational places, and 2.2% in others, which includes military bases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Template:Refexample#Self-sourcing" title="Template:Refexample"><span title="This example needs references to sources that describe its significance in the context of the article. (October 2018)">betterÂ sourceÂ needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (October 2018)">relevant?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>
