Other carcinogens identified in food include <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_amine" title="Heterocyclic amine">heterocyclic amines</a> generated in meat when cooked at high temperature, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyaromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polyaromatic hydrocarbon">polyaromatic hydrocarbons</a> in charred meat and smoked fish, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">nitrosamines</a> generated from nitrites used as food preservatives in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cured_meat" title="Cured meat">cured meat</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Bacon" title="Bacon">bacon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Anticarcinogen" title="Anticarcinogen">Anticarcinogens</a> that may help prevent cancer can also be found in many food especially fruit and vegetables. <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidants</a> are important groups of compounds that may help remove potentially harmful chemicals. It is however often difficult to identify the specific components in diet that serve to increase or decrease cancer risk since many food, such as beef steak and broccoli, contain low concentrations of both carcinogens and anticarcinogens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-carcinogens_and_anticarcinogens-196">[196]</a></sup>
There are many international certifications in the cooking field, such as <a href="/wiki/Monde_Selection" title="Monde Selection">Monde Selection</a>, A.A. Certification, iTQi. They use high-quality evaluation methods to make the food safer.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FAO_kcal_his.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="625" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/300px-FAO_kcal_his.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/450px-FAO_kcal_his.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/FAO_kcal_his.png/600px-FAO_kcal_his.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FAO_kcal_his.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Changes of food supply (by energy)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><br/>Other area (Yr 2010)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> * Africa, sub-Sahara - 2170 kcal/capita/day * N.E. and N. Africa - 3120 kcal/capita/day * South Asia - 2450 kcal/capita/day * East Asia - 3040 kcal/capita/day * Latin America / Caribbean - 2950 kcal/capita/day * Developed countries - 3470 kcal/capita/day</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">Diet (nutrition)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_and_religious_diets">Cultural and religious diets</span></h3>
<p>Many cultures hold some food preferences and some food <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboos</a>. Dietary choices can also define cultures and play a role in religion. For example, only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosher" title="Kosher">kosher foods</a> are permitted by <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a> foods by <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> is restricted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> In addition, the dietary choices of different countries or regions have different characteristics. This is highly related to a culture's <a href="/wiki/Cuisine" title="Cuisine">cuisine</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet_deficiencies">Diet deficiencies</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avitaminosis" title="Avitaminosis">Avitaminosis</a></div>
<p>Dietary habits play a significant role in the health and mortality of all humans. Imbalances between the consumed fuels and expended energy results in either starvation or excessive reserves of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adipose" title="Adipose">adipose</a> tissue, known as body fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> Poor intake of various vitamins and minerals can lead to diseases that can have far-reaching effects on health. For instance, 30% of the world's population either has, or is at risk for developing, <a href="/wiki/Iodine_deficiency" title="Iodine deficiency">iodine deficiency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> It is estimated that at least 3 million children are blind due to <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">vitamin A</a> deficiency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">Vitamin C</a> deficiency results in <a href="/wiki/Scurvy" title="Scurvy">scurvy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">Calcium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">Vitamin D</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> are inter-related; the consumption of each may affect the absorption of the others. <a href="/wiki/Kwashiorkor" title="Kwashiorkor">Kwashiorkor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marasmus" title="Marasmus">marasmus</a> are childhood disorders caused by lack of dietary protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Moral.2C_ethical.2C_and_health-conscious_diets"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Moral,_ethical,_and_health-conscious_diets">Moral, ethical, and health-conscious diets</span></h3>
<p>Many individuals limit what foods they eat for reasons of morality or other habits. For instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarians</a> choose to forgo food from animal sources to varying degrees. Others choose a <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet" title="Healthy diet">healthier diet</a>, avoiding sugars or animal fats and increasing consumption of dietary fiber and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">Obesity</a>, a serious problem in the western world, leads to higher chances of developing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, cancer and many other diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> More recently, dietary habits have been influenced by the concerns that some people have about possible impacts on health or the environment from <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">genetically modified food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Further concerns about the impact of industrial farming (<a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">grains</a>) on <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a>, human health, and the <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">environment</a> are also having an effect on contemporary human dietary habits. This has led to the emergence of a movement with a preference for <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">organic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Local_food" title="Local food">local food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Sweetness" title="Sweetness">sweetness</a> multiplier "300 times" comes from <a class="external text" href="http://www.science.edu.sg/ssc/detailed.jsp?artid=67&amp;type=6&amp;root=3&amp;parent=3&amp;cat=30" rel="nofollow">subjective evaluations by a panel of test subjects</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090123124043/http://www.science.edu.sg/ssc/detailed.jsp?artid=67&amp;type=6&amp;root=3&amp;parent=3&amp;cat=30" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 23 January 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> tasting various dilutions compared to a standard dilution of <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>. Sources referenced in this article say steviosides have up to 250 times the sweetness of sucrose, but others, including stevioside brands such as SweetLeaf, claim 300 times. 1/3 to 1/2 teaspoon (1.6â2.5 ml) of stevioside powder is claimed to have equivalent sweetening power to 1 cup (237 ml) of sugar.</span>
</li>

(of food, esp. milk) spoiled because of fermentation." New Oxford American Dictionary</span>

<li>Mobbs, Michael (2012). <i>Sustainable Food</i> Sydney: NewSouth Publishing,  <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-920705-54-1" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-920705-54-1">978-1-920705-54-1</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VQsC7e2qAcY" rel="nofollow">The Future of Food</a> (2015).  A panel discussion at the 2015  <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Life_Design" title="Digital Life Design">Digital Life Design</a> (DLD)</i> Annual Conference. "How can we grow and enjoy food, closer to home, further into the future? <i><a href="/wiki/MIT_Media_Lab" title="MIT Media Lab">MIT Media Lab</a>'s</i> Kevin Slavin hosts a conversation with food artist, educator, and entrepreneur Emilie Baltz, professor Caleb Harper from <i>MIT Media Lab's</i> CityFarm project, the Barbarian Group's Benjamin Palmer, and Andras Forgacs, the co-founder and CEO of <i>Modern Meadow</i>, who is growing 'victimless' meat in a lab. The discussion addresses issues of sustainable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_farming" title="Urban farming">urban farming</a>, ecosystems, technology, food supply chains and their broad environmental and humanitarian implications, and how these changes in food production may change what people may find delicious ... and the other way around."  Posted on the official YouTube Channel of <i><a href="/wiki/Digital_Life_Design" title="Digital Life Design">DLD</a></i></li></ul>
